template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Irreligion</b> (adjective form: <i>non-religious</i> or <i>irreligious</i>) is the absence, indifference, rejection of, or hostility towards <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Irreligion may include some forms of <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">theism</a>, depending on the religious context it is defined against; for example, in 18th-century Europe, the <a href="/wiki/Epitome" title="Epitome">epitome</a> of irreligion was <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> while in contemporary <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a> the shared term meaning "irreligion" or "no religion" (\xe7\x84\xa1\xe5\xae\x97\xe6\x95\x99, Chinese pron. <i>w\xc3\xba z\xc5\x8dngji\xc3\xa0o</i>, Japanese pron. <i>mu sh\xc5\xabky\xc5\x8d</i>), with which the majority of East Asian populations identify themselves, implies non-membership in one of the institutional religions (such as Buddhism and Christianity), and not necessarily non-belief in traditional folk religions collectively represented by Chinese <i><a href="/wiki/Shendao" class="mw-redirect" title="Shendao">Shendao</a></i> and Japanese <i><a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a></i> (both meaning "ways of gods").<sup id="cite_ref-Handbook_Japanese_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Handbook_Japanese-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a>\'s 2012 global study of 230 countries and territories, 16% of the world\'s population is not affiliated with a religion, while 84% are affiliated.<sup id="cite_ref-Pew_Global_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew_Global-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> By 2060, according to their projections, the number of unaffiliated will increase by over 35 million, but the percentage will decrease to 13% because the total population will grow faster.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to cross cultural studies, secularism is expected to decline throughout the 21st century since religion and fertility are positively related, while secularism and fertility are negatively related.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Kinds_of_irreligion"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Kinds of irreligion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#Human_rights"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Human rights</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-3"><a href="#Non-denominational"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Non-denominational</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-4"><a href="#Demographics"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Demographics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-6"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-7"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Kinds_of_irreligion">Kinds of irreligion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irreligion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Kinds of irreligion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Secular_humanism" title="Secular humanism">Secular humanism</a> embraces human reason, ethics, social justice, and philosophical naturalism while specifically rejecting religious dogma, supernaturalism, pseudoscience, and superstition as the bases of morality and decision making. Secular humanism posits that human beings are capable of being ethical and moral without religion or a god.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Freethought" title="Freethought">Freethought</a> holds that positions regarding truth should be formed on the basis of logic, reason, and empiricism, rather than authority, tradition, revelation, or other dogma. In particular, freethought is strongly tied with rejection of traditional religious belief.</li>\n<li>"<a href="/wiki/Spiritual_but_not_religious" title="Spiritual but not religious">Spiritual but not religious</a>" rejects organized religion as the sole or most valuable means of furthering spiritual growth. In contrast to religion, spirituality has often been associated with the interior life of the individual.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Theological_noncognitivism" title="Theological noncognitivism">Theological noncognitivism</a> is the argument that religious language \xe2\x80\x93 specifically, words such as "God" \xe2\x80\x93 are not cognitively meaningful. It is sometimes considered as synonymous with <a href="/wiki/Ignosticism" title="Ignosticism">ignosticism</a>.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Antireligion" title="Antireligion">Antireligion</a> is opposition to religion of any kind. It can describe opposition to organized religion, religious practices, religious institutions, or specific forms of supernatural worship or practice, whether organized or not.</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_rights">Human rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irreligion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Human rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In 1993, the UN\'s human rights committee declared that article 18 of the <a href="/wiki/International_Covenant_on_Civil_and_Political_Rights" title="International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights">International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights</a> "protects theistic, non-theistic and atheistic beliefs, as well as the right not to profess any religion or belief."<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The committee further stated that "the freedom to have or to adopt a religion or belief necessarily entails the freedom to choose a religion or belief, including the right to replace one\'s current religion or belief with another or to adopt atheistic views." Signatories to the convention are barred from "the use of threat of physical force or penal sanctions to compel believers or non-believers" to recant their beliefs or convert.<sup id="cite_ref-fdih1_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fdih1-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most Western democracies protect the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion" title="Freedom of religion">freedom of religion</a>, and it is largely implied in respective legal systems that those who do not believe or observe any religion are allowed <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_thought" title="Freedom of thought">freedom of thought</a>.\n</p><p>A noted exception to ambiguity, explicitly allowing non-religion, is Article 36 of the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Constitution of the People&#39;s Republic of China">Constitution of the People\'s Republic of China</a> (as adopted in 1982), which states that "No state organ, public organization or individual may compel citizens to believe in, or not to believe in, any religion; nor may they discriminate against citizens who believe in, or do not believe in, any religion."<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Article 46 of China\xe2\x80\x99s <a href="/wiki/1978_Constitution_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="1978 Constitution of the People&#39;s Republic of China">1978 Constitution</a> was even more explicit, stating that "Citizens enjoy freedom to believe in religion and freedom not to believe in religion and to propagate atheism."<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-denominational">Non-denominational</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irreligion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Non-denominational">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>A non-denominational person or organization is not restricted to any particular or specific <a href="/wiki/Religious_denomination" title="Religious denomination">religious denomination</a>. The term has been used in the context of various faiths including <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="Bah\xc3\xa1&#39;\xc3\xad Faith">Bah\xc3\xa1\'\xc3\xad Faith</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Unitarian_Universalism" title="Unitarian Universalism">Unitarian Universalism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">Paganism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Pollack_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pollack-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Wicca" title="Wicca">Wicca</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> It stands in contrast with a <a href="/wiki/Religious_denomination" title="Religious denomination">religious denomination</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irreligion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_irreligion" title="List of countries by irreligion">List of countries by irreligion</a></div>\n<p>Although 11 countries listed below have non-religious majorities, it does not mean that the majority of the populations of these countries don\xe2\x80\xb2t belong to any religious group. For example, 68% of the <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Swedish</a> population belongs to the Lutheran Christian Church,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> while 59% of <a href="/wiki/Albanians" title="Albanians">Albanians</a> declare themselves as Muslims.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Also, though Scandinavian countries have among the highest measures of nonreligiosity and even atheism in Europe, 47% of atheists who live in those countries are still members of the national churches.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A Pew 2015 global projection study for religion and nonreligion, projects that between 2010 and 2050, there will be some initial increases of the unaffiliated followed by a decline by 2050 due to lower global fertility rates among this demographic.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Sociologist <a href="/wiki/Phil_Zuckerman" title="Phil Zuckerman">Phil Zuckerman</a>\'s global studies on atheism have indicated that global atheism may be in decline due to irreligious countries having the lowest birth rates in the world and religious countries having higher birth rates in general.<sup id="cite_ref-zucker_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zucker-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to Pew Research Center\'s 2012 global study of 230 countries and territories, 16% of the world\'s population is not affiliated with a religion, while 84% are affiliated.<sup id="cite_ref-Pew_Global_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew_Global-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> A 2012 <a href="/wiki/WIN/GIA" title="WIN/GIA">Worldwide Independent Network/Gallup International Association</a> report on a poll from 57 countries reported that 59% of the world\'s population identified as religious person, 23% as not religious person, 13% as "convinced <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheists</a>", and also a 9% decrease in identification as "religious" when compared to the 2005 average from 39 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-GallupInt2012_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GallupInt2012-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Their follow-up report, based on a poll in 2015, found that 63% of the globe identified as religious person, 22% as not religious person, and 11% as "convinced atheists".<sup id="cite_ref-GallupInt2015_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GallupInt2015-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Their 2017 report found that 62% of the globe identified as religious person, 25% as not religious person, and 9% as "convinced atheists".<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> However, researchers have advised caution with the WIN/Gallup International figures since other surveys which use the same wording, have conducted many waves for decades, and have a bigger sample size, such as World Values Survey; have consistently reached lower figures for the number of atheists worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Being non-religious is not necessarily equivalent to being an atheist or agnostic. Pew Research Center\'s global study from 2012 noted that many of the nonreligious actually have some religious beliefs. For example, they observed that "belief in God or a higher power is shared by 7% of Chinese unaffiliated adults, 30% of French unaffiliated adults and 68% of unaffiliated U.S. adults."<sup id="cite_ref-Pew_Global_Unaffiliated_12/2012_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew_Global_Unaffiliated_12/2012-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Out of the global nonreligious population, 76% reside in Asia and the Pacific, while the remainder reside in Europe (12%), North America (5%), Latin America and the Caribbean (4%), sub-Saharan Africa (2%) and the Middle East and North Africa (less than 1%).<sup id="cite_ref-Pew_Global_Unaffiliated_12/2012_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew_Global_Unaffiliated_12/2012-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The term <b>nones</b> is sometimes used in the U.S. to refer to those who are unaffiliated with any organized religion. This use derives from surveys of religious affiliation, in which "None" (or "None of the above") is typically the last choice. Since this status refers to lack of organizational affiliation rather than lack of personal belief, it is a more specific concept than irreligion. A 2015 <a href="/wiki/Gallup_poll" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallup poll">Gallup poll</a> concluded that in the U.S. "nones" were the only "religious" group that was growing as a percentage of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-gallup_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gallup-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable" cellpadding="0">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Encyclopedia+of+Religion+and+Society&amp;rft.atitle=Irreligion&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fhirr.hartsem.edu%2Fency%2Firreligion.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIrreligion" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/> Includes <i>rejection</i>.</li>\n<li><cite id="CITEREFColin_Campbell" class="citation">Colin Campbell, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=6TMFoMFe-D8C&amp;pg=PA239">"Irreligion"</a>, <i>Encyclopedia of Religion and Society</i><span class="reference-accessdate">, retrieved <span class="nowrap">2012-02-18</span></span></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Encyclopedia+of+Religion+and+Society&amp;rft.atitle=Irreligion&amp;rft.au=Colin+Campbell&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D6TMFoMFe-D8C%26pg%3DPA239&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIrreligion" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></li></ul>\n</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Campbell, Colin. 1971. <i>Towards a Sociology of Irreligion</i>. London:McMillan p. 31.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Handbook_Japanese-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Handbook_Japanese_3-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Bestor, Theodore C.; Bestor, Victoria; Yamagata, Akiko, eds. (2011). <i>Handbook of Japanese Culture and Society</i>. London: Routledge. pp.&#160;66\xe2\x80\x9367. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0415436494" title="Special:BookSources/0415436494">0415436494</a>. <q>\xe7\x84\xa1\xe5\xae\x97\xe6\x95\x99 <i>mush\xc5\xabky\xc5\x8d</i>, "no religion", in Japanese language and mindset identifies those people who do not belong to organised religion. To the Japanese, the term "religion" or "faith" means organised religions on the model of Christianity, that is a religion with specific doctrines and requirement for church membership. So, when asked "what is their religion", most of the Japanese answer that they "do not belong to any religion". According to <a href="/wiki/NHK" title="NHK">NHK</a> studies, those Japanese who identify with <i>mush\xc5\xabky\xc5\x8d</i> and therefore do not belong to any organised religion, actually take part in the folk ritual dimension of <i>Shinto</i>. Ama Toshimaru in <i>Nihonjin wa naze mushukyo na no ka</i> ("Why are the Japanese non-religious?") of 1996, explains that people who do not belong to organised religions but regularly pray and make offerings to ancestors and protective deities at private altars or Shinto shrines will identify themselves as <i>mushukyo</i>. Ama designates "natural religion" what NHK studies define as "folk religion", and other scholars have named "Nipponism" (<i>Nipponky\xc5\x8d</i>) or "common religion".</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Handbook+of+Japanese+Culture+and+Society&amp;rft.place=London&amp;rft.pages=66-67&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.isbn=0415436494&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIrreligion" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Pew_Global-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Pew_Global_4-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Pew_Global_4-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Pew Forum on Religion &amp; Public Life. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.pewforum.org/2012/12/18/global-religious-landscape-exec/">"The Global Religious Landscape"</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">December 18,</span> 2012</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=The+Global+Religious+Landscape&amp;rft.au=Pew+Forum+on+Religion+%26+Public+Life&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.pewforum.org%2F2012%2F12%2F18%2Fglobal-religious-landscape-exec%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIrreligion" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.pewresearch.org/fact-tank/2015/04/03/why-people-with-no-religion-are-projected-to-decline-as-a-share-of-the-worlds-population/">"Why People With No Religion Are Projected To Decline As A Share Of The World\'s Population"</a>. Pew Research Center.
